   temperature in the Northern Hemisphere of -90oF. Snag, in the Yukon Territory of Canada,
   holds the North American record of -81oF. Arctic coastal areas in the winter usually average
   about 20oF warmer than the interior due to the modifying effect of water and ice. During
   summer, some areas are surprisingly warm. The interior areas have pleasantly warm
   summers with many hours of sunshine each day. Figure 15.3 shows the average number of
   days each year with above freezing temperatures. Continental interior temperatures
   sometimes rise to the high 70s or 80s and occasionally into the 90s. Fort Yukon, located just
   north of the Arctic Circle, has recorded l00oF; Verkhoyansk has recorded 94oF. Arctic coastal
   areas have cool, short summers tempered by the persistent onshore winds and abundant
   cloudiness. Summer high temperatures occasionally reach the 60s with low temperatures
   consistently dipping below freezing. For example, during July, Barrow Point in northern
   Alaska has an average temperature of 40°F.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                  175


Figure 15.3. Average Number of Days with Temperatures above Freezing.




   15.5.2. Cloudiness over the Arctic is at a minimum in winter and at a maximum in summer
   and fall. Coastal locations are particularly affected by prolonged periods of stratocumulus
   ceilings during the summer and early fall because of onshore flow from the Arctic Ocean.
   Scattered cumulus clouds form over interior regions during warm summer afternoons.
   Occasionally the interior experiences low-topped thunderstorms with the typical associated
   thunderstorm aviation hazards. Even a tornado or two has been observed in the vicinity of the
   Arctic Circle although they are extremely rare.
   15.5.3. Precipitation (usually snow) in the Arctic is generally light. Annual amounts over the
   ice pack and along the coastal area are only 3 to 7 inches. Coastal areas with sharp, rising
   terrain and the interior areas are somewhat wetter, with annual amounts of 5 to 15 inches,
   mostly consisting of summer rain.
15.6. Winds.
   15.6.1. Strong winds are more frequent during the fall and winter, especially along the
   coastal areas. A number of coastal stations have recorded wind speeds greater than 70 kts.
   Along the coast of Greenland, winds in excess of 90 kts are not uncommon in winter. Strong
   down-slope (fall) winds are very hazardous to aircrews. As these cold winds drain from high
   plateau or mountain areas down to lower elevations, their speed increases abruptly and may
   exceed 100 kts. With the lack of vegetation, the winds blow constantly at many locations
   with the speed averaging between 10 and 20 knots.
   15.6.2. Strong winds combined with low temperatures make the Arctic very uncomfortable
   for human activity. The combination produces wind chill temperatures of -l00oF or colder.
   This is computed according to the average wind speed without gusts with the free air
   temperature. The wind chill temperature is the sensed temperature on the human body taking
   into account the additional cooling effect of wind on the human body. It is the temperature
 176                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


   that equates the same rate of cooling under calm wind conditions. Figure 15.4 is a chart used
   in computing wind chill factors.

Figure 15.4. Wind Chill Index.




15.7. Temperature.
   15.7.1. The rapid increase in temperature with height at low levels over the Polar Regions
   during much of the winter causes interesting phenomena. Sound tends to carry great
   distances under these inversions. Light rays are bent as they pass through the inversion at low
   angles. This may cause the appearance of objects that are actually below the horizon. This
   effect, known as looming, is a mirage. Mirages that distort the apparent shape of the sun,
   moon, and other objects are common with these inversions.
15.8. Aurora.
   15.8.1. Disturbances on the sun spew out bursts of energetic particles, a portion of which
   eventually reach the Earth. The high-energy particles strike the Earth’s magnetic field and are
   carried along the magnetic field lines of the Earth where they tend to lower and converge
   near the geomagnetic poles. The electrons pass through rarefied gases of the outer
   atmosphere, illuminating similarly as a fluorescent lamp. The illuminated gases, more
   commonly known as the northern lights or the aurora borealis (Figure 15.5), have their
   counterpart in the Antarctic, where they are termed the aurora australis or southern lights.
   While the northern lights are frequently observed in the Arctic region, they have been seen as
   far south as Florida. Displays of aurora vary from a faint glow to that of a full moon. The
   lights frequently change shape and form. The most predominant color is pale green, but
   yellow, white, red, blue, and violet are sometimes observed. When auroras are active, high
   frequency (HF) radio communications may experience increased noise levels. At times, the
   noise levels are severe enough to totally degrade HF radio reception. The interference, which
   sounds like continuous static, lasts from 30 minutes to a few hours.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                   177


Figure 15.5. Aurora Borealis.




15.9. Visual Complications in Arctic Environment.
   15.9.1. Snow-covered surfaces reflect much more light than darker surfaces. Snow often
   reflects Arctic sunlight sufficiently to blot out shadows. This markedly decreases the contrast
   between objects, and it becomes very difficult to distinguish between them. The landscape
   may merge into a featureless grayish-white field. Dark mountains in the distance may be
   easily recognized, but a crevasse normally directly in view may be undetected due to lack of
   contrast.
   15.9.2. Illumination from the stars creates visibility far beyond that found elsewhere. Only
   during heavy overcasts does the Arctic night begin to approach the degree of darkness in
   lower latitudes. There are often long periods of moonlight, with the moon staying above the
   horizon for several days at a time.
   15.9.3. For the most part, fewer severe weather hazards exist in the polar regions for aircraft
   at flight altitudes than in other regions. But parts of Greenland compete with the Aleutian
   Islands for the world’s worst weather. During winter, many coastal and adjacent inland
   locations are frequently affected by synoptic storms which originate from the Gulf of Alaska
   and other similar semi-permanent low pressure areas. The interior is not quite as affected
   because of the dryness of the air and more stable dominant air masses. Expect turbulence
   especially in mountainous areas and flat areas adjacent to mountainous areas subject to
   strong pressure gradients. Strong channeled winds can cause moderate to severe low level
   turbulence below 5,000 feet.
   15.9.4. Most Arctic air masses exhibit a pronounced dryness in all but the lowest layers.
   Occasionally, moisture works its way into the upper levels in maritime polar air masses.
   This, in turn, sets the stage for icing conditions. Icing can be encountered when passing
   through seemingly safe clouds of thin consistency. Rime icing is the dominant form of icing
   with some mixed icing forming along coastal mountain areas.
15.10. Arctic Whiteout.
   15.10.1. Whiteout is a visibility-restricting phenomenon occurring in the polar regions when
   a uniformly overcast layer of clouds overlies a snow or ice covered surface. Most whiteouts
   occur when the cloud deck is relatively low and the sun is about 20o above the horizon.
 178                                                         AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


   15.10.2. The parallel rays from the sun are broken up and diffused when passing through the
   cloud layer so that they strike the snow surface from many angles (Figure 15.6). This
   diffused light reflects back and forth countless times between the snow and clouds until all
   shadows are eliminated. The result is a loss of depth perception. Low level flight and
   landings on snow surfaces become dangerous. Several disastrous aircraft crashes have
   occurred where whiteout conditions may have been a factor.

Figure 15.6. Whiteout Condition.




15.11. Restrictions to Flying.
   15.11.1. There are several causes of reduced visibility in arctic regions. Aircrews flying over
   polar regions sometimes experience reduced horizontal and slant visibilities without any
   visible clouds or precipitation. This phenomenon is caused by extremely small ice particles in
   the atmosphere. It is also called Arctic mist when near the ground. Fog limits takeoff and
   landing in the polar regions more than any other visibility restriction. Water droplet fog is the
   main hazard to aircraft operators in coastal areas during the summer. It is a potential source
   of aircraft icing when temperatures are below freezing. Ice fog, a major operational hazard, is
   common in polar areas in winter. It forms in moist air during extremely cold weather.
   Combustion of aircraft fuel in air -20oF (-29oC) and colder produces the ice fog most
   frequently affecting aircraft operations. In supersaturated conditions, routine aircraft engine
   running or movement can supply enough exhaust impurities and moisture to cause
   deposition. The resulting ice fog may restrict visibilities enough to halt aircraft operations at
   the airfield for hours. Blowing snow is a greater hazard to flying operations in polar regions
   than in mid-latitudes because the snow is dry and fine and is easily picked up by light winds
   (Figure 15.7). Winds may raise the snow 1,000 feet AGL and produce drifts more than 30
   feet deep, obscuring objects such as runway markers. Under certain conditions, a frequent
   and sudden increase in surface winds may cause visibility to drop from unlimited to near zero
   within a few minutes (Figure 15.8). Blowing snow is deceptive to inexperienced aircrews
   since the shallowness of the snow layer may permit good vertical visibility at the same time
   that the horizontal visibility within the layer is very poor.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                  179


Figure 15.7. Blowing Snow.




Figure 15.8. Visibility Reduced in Blowing Snow.




   15.11.2. In winter, temperatures may increase from -60°F at the surface to 0°F only 1,500
   feet above the ground. Such a strong temperature inversion will dramatically decrease the
   climb performance of an aircraft. Anticipate them and be alert when the weather forecaster
   briefs the magnitude of the inversion. Then follow procedures listed in your flight manual for
   cold weather operation. When the mean actual temperature is much colder than a standard
   column of air, the distance between two flight levels will be less than the indicated altitude
   difference. Since the colder air shrinks the distance between two pressure levels, the actual
   vertical difference can be 80% of the distance when compared to a standard air column. For
180                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


  example, when the vertical separation distance is expected to be 2,000 feet in a standard
  atmosphere, in extremely cold temperatures, the vertical distance can shrink to 1,600 feet.
  15.11.3. Strong winds over rough terrain and very low temperatures are sometimes
  responsible for large errors in altimetry. Chapter 4 and the following section discuss the
  cause and effect of these errors. Aircrews should allow for an ample safety margin in
  selecting flight altitudes over mountainous terrain. Be alert when flying over extremely cold
  areas where the total air column is dramatically colder than a standard column of air. The
  actual temperature deviation will be dramatically different than the standard temperature.
  Safe terrain clearance is adversely affected by cold temperatures. The magnitude of this
  difference can become quite large during cold, winter conditions when flight is undertaken
  over mountains in cases where the altimeter setting is obtained from a station whose
  elevation is well below that of the highest peak to be crossed by an aircraft. The altimeter
  setting reported by a station at the base of the mountain will be higher than the actual
  altimeter setting on top of the mountain. If flying in the vicinity of a mountain top and you
  have your altimeter setting adjusted to a station setting at the base of the mountain, and the
  actual mean temperature is below the standard mean temperature, the indicated altitude
  shown by the aircraft instrument will be higher than the true altitude. This is a dangerous
  situation. The indicated altimeter setting can read as much as 1,500 feet too high. When the
  actual mean temperature exceeds that of a standard air column, then the altimeter setting at
  the mountain top will be higher than that of the station altimeter setting at the base of the
  mountain. The indicated altitude shown by the aircraft instrument will be lower than the true
  altitude.
  15.11.4. In some parts of the United States such as the western plateau areas of Colorado,
  Montana, Idaho, Utah, and Wyoming, minimum temperatures of -30°F have been observed
  while in Alaska surface temperatures can be -50°F. It is not uncommon for weather stations
  to be located along the side of mountain peaks or ridges whose tops rise from 5,000 to 9,000
  feet above the elevation of the stations. In some regions such as Greenland, the air traffic
  controller will issue a temperature corrected altitude for aircraft flying in the region during
  extreme cold temperature situations.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                     181


                                           Chapter 16

                                        VOLCANIC ASH

16.1. Introduction. Volcanic activity was not generally considered a severe threat to aircraft
until the beginning of the 1980’s. However, the first recorded volcanic eruption which had any
appreciable impact on aviation occurred 22 March 1944 when Mount Vesuvius in Italy did more
damage on an Allied airfield than would have likely happened from an enemy raid. The
American 340th Bombardment Group’s entire fleet of 88 North American B-25 Mitchells had
their fabric control surfaces burned off and all Plexiglas pitted. The weight of ash which fell on
the aircraft at their field near Naples, tipped them onto their tails. All 88 were severely damaged.
It would be 36 years until another volcano would have so significant an impact on aviation. Why
such a long time? Simply because we entered a period of about three decades in which there
were very few explosive eruptions of the world’s volcanoes. According to Decker and Decker in
their book Volcanoes, there are about 40 subduction volcanoes (the type most often explosive)
erupting each year. But during this period the Earth’s volcanoes were uncharacteristically quiet.
16.2. History.
   16.2.1. Since the early 1980’s there have been several major volcanic eruptions around the
   world. These eruptions have given aircraft manufacturers and aviators more than they
   bargained for. Beginning in 1980 with the eruption of Mount Saint Helens in Washington
   state (Figure 16l), a new chapter began in the story of ―aviation versus volcanoes.‖ The
   decade held at least two major aviation encounters with volcanic ash which almost resulted in
   loss of life near Indonesia and adversely affected numerous flying operations in Alaska. Take
   a moment to re-live the following aircraft encounter with a dangerous volcanic ash cloud.
   Unfortunately this is but one incident; there have been many others. After reading the next
   few paragraphs, your concern and respect for volcanic phenomenon should be greatly
   enhanced.
 182                                                       AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


Figure 16.1. Mount St Helens, May 1980.




16.3. Case Study.
   16.3.1. In early summer of 1982, a British Airways B-747 with 247 passengers and 16 crew
   members was enroute from Kuala Lumpur, Malaysia to Perth, Australia. It was cruising at
   37,000 feet. The onboard radar was painting nothing unusual. The captain had left the flight
   deck. The eerie blue glow of St. Elmo’s Fire on windscreens and engine nacelles (coverings)
   was the first sign of trouble. The crew turned on anti-ice and igniters on all engines and
   switched on the seat belt lights in the passenger compartment as a precaution. Turning on the
   landing lights, the first officer peered out into the darkness. It seemed to him the plane was
   passing through a continuous, thin cloud.
   16.3.2. The captain was called to return to the cockpit. Soon the crew noticed what seemed
   to be a bluish smoke on the flight deck and the acrid smell of ozone. A thin shaft of light
   projecting forward of the plane’s four Rolls-Royce RB.211 engines appeared, somewhat
   similar to a flashlight’s beam. Passengers began noticing the blue smoke in their cabin and
   became uneasy. Back in the cockpit, a light glowed on the flight engineer’s panel indicating
   the number four engine’s air supply was down. Within a short time the engine’s rpm and
   pressure ratio began to drop and it wound to a halt. The crew began the engine fire checklist
   for number four but as it was completed, the number two engine also began to run down. The
   remaining two engines did the same. Now, all four engines were out! The plane was 80
   nautical miles from land, 180 miles from a suitable runway.
   16.3.3. To attempt to restart the engines would require the crew to maintain an indicated
   airspeed of 250 to 270 knots. At the same time, the aircraft was descending at a rate of 2,000
   feet per minute. That meant a water landing in 18 minutes, time to glide perhaps halfway to
   Jakarta, Indonesia. Naturally the flight engineer worked continuously to restart the engines.
   By this time the plane was vibrating vigorously and flames were issuing from the tailpipes
   due to the restart attempts. In the cabin, passengers were listening to emergency ditching
   instructions.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                   183


   16.3.4. Eight minutes after the first engine failure, as the plane descended through 13,000
   feet, the number four engine relit. That helped save the day, because if just one engine stayed
   operational, the plane could slow its descent rate or maybe even maintain altitude. Over the
   next 5 minutes, the crew was able to get the remaining three engines relit and start a climb.
   At 15,000 feet however, the St. Elmo’s fire was again observed and the crew began another
   descent. The number two engine stopped again and was shut down. The plane proceeded on
   to Jakarta on three engines and made an uneventful landing. The front windshields were so
   badly scratched and the landing lights rendered so useless that the crew requested the plane
   be towed off the runway after landing.
   16.3.5. This incident was the result of a cloud of volcanic ash from Java’s Galunggung
   volcano which had erupted about an hour before the B-747 left Kuala Lumpur. Analyzing the
   flight data recorders, engineers learned that the four engines had begun to lose power
   imperceptibly about 5 minutes before the shutdowns began. The plane gradually lost speed
   and the autopilot increased the pitch angle to maintain the assigned altitude until the first
   engine failure and long descent began. Inspection of the engines revealed light erosion of the
   cowl leading edges and pieces of fused volcanic ash debris in the tailpipes. After being torn
   down, the engines were found to have various degrees of erosion. In the hot sections, fused
   volcanic dust had built up on the walls and blades. Outside the plane, sandblasting (ash-
   blasting) had eroded the central portions of the windshields and frosted over the landing
   lights. This harrowing experience is a good example of hazards faced by aircrews resulting
   from unreported volcanic events and from resulting ash clouds which often cannot be
   detected.
16.4. Avoiding Volcanic Ash.
   16.4.1. The ash plume may not be visible, especially in instrument conditions or at night,
   and even if visible it is difficult to distinguish between an ash cloud and an ordinary weather
   cloud. Volcanic ash clouds are not displayed on airborne or Air Traffic Control (ATC) radar.
   In fact, radar reflectivity of volcanic ash is roughly a million times less than that of a
   cumuliform cloud. Pilots must rely on reports from controllers and other pilots to determine
   the location of the ash cloud and use that information to remain well clear of the area. If you
   see a volcanic eruption and have not been previously notified of it, you may be the first
   person to observe it--especially in remote locations. In this case, immediately contact ATC
   personnel and alert them to the hazard. Remember, you are the most important link in
   volcano observation and warning programs for aviators.
   16.4.2. When landing at airports where volcanic ash has been deposited on the runway, be
   aware that even a thin layer of dry ash can be detrimental to braking action. Wet ash can also
   impair your braking action. It is recommended that reverse thrust be limited to minimum
   practical to lessen the possibility of reduced visibility and engine ingestion of airborne ash.
   When departing from affected airfields, you should avoid operating in visible airborne ash. It
   is also recommended that flap extension be delayed until initiating the before takeoff
   checklist and that a rolling takeoff be executed to avoid blowing ash back into the air.
   16.4.3. What happens to a turbo jet engine which causes such severe and costly damage?
   After all, these engines have a long history of operating in desert areas such as the American
   Southwest where a high level of dust in the atmosphere is commonplace. In Kuwait, fine-
   grained dust is said to exist suspended in the atmosphere up to 15,000 feet. The usual
 184                                                       AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


   experience is that such dust causes slow erosion of the engine’s compressor blades but no
   case of major engine failure is known to have resulted from these conditions. Volcanic dust
   can cause rapid erosion and damage to the internal components of engines. As it builds up,
   blockage of the high pressure turbine nozzle guide vanes and high pressure turbine cooling
   holes can cause surge, loss of thrust and/or high exhaust gas temperature. Because the
   greatest constituent of volcanic ash is silicon, it forms a glazing on the hot turbine
   components. The ash is also highly abrasive and may cause serious damage not only to the
   engines, but to leading wing edges, windshields, and landing lights as well. The ash may also
   block the pitot static system causing unreliable airspeed indications.
16.5. Volcano Reporting.
   16.5.1. Since 1982, the International Civil Aviation Organization (ICAO) has worked to
   address the volcanic threat to aviation safety worldwide. In the United States, the US
   Geological Survey is the principle federal agency with responsibility for assessing volcanic
   hazards and monitoring active volcanoes. This work is carried out primarily from
   observatories in Hawaii, Alaska, California, Washington and the nine regional Volcanic Ash
   Advisory Centers (VAACs) around the world. Volcanic Ash Advisory Centers (VAAC) are a
   group of experts responsible for coordinating and disseminating information on atmospheric
   volcanic ash clouds that may endanger aviation. As of 2010, there are nine Volcanic Ash
   Advisory Centers located around the world, each one focusing on a particular geographical
   region. Their analyses are made public in the form of Volcanic Ash Advisories (VAA) and
   often incorporate the results of computer simulation models called Volcanic Ash Transport
   and Dispersion (VATD). The worldwide network of Volcanic Ash Advisory Centers was set
   up by the ICAO, an agency of the United Nations. The individual VAACs are run as part of
   national weather forecasting organizations of the country where they are based, e.g. the US
   National Oceanic and Atmospheric Administration (NOAA) or the British Met Office.
   NOAA and the FAA have responsibilities for dealing with the hazard of volcanic ash clouds
   that affect aviation operations in the United States. Methods used for assessing volcanic
   activity include analysis of data from satellites, upper-air wind sensors, lightning detection
   systems, pilot reports, and even specialized radars. Computer models have been developed to
   help forecast the trajectory of volcanic ash clouds so that Significant Meteorological
   Information (SIGMETs) and other timely products may be issued to warn aviators of this
   potentially lethal hazard. Figure 16.2 is an advisory product showing the transport and
   dispersion of an ash plume from the December 2010 eruption of Fuego in Guatemala. The
   figure shows the flight levels at which the ash plume may have be encountered along flight
   routes over the area. Figure 16.3 is a dispersion visualization chart produced by the Air
   Force Weather Agency (AFWA) showing the dispersion forecast for Sakura-Jima near
   Kyushu, Japan in August 2011.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                   185


Figure 16.2. Volcanic Ash Advisory Product.
 186                                                       AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


Figure 16.3. AFWA Volcanic Ash Dispersion Visualization.




   16.5.2. Aircrew Notes.
       16.5.2.1. It is recommended that pilots encountering an ash cloud immediately reduce
       thrust to idle (altitude permitting) and reverse course in order to escape from the cloud.
       Ash clouds may extend for hundreds of miles so pilots should not attempt to fly through
       or climb out of the cloud. The following procedures are recommended. Some of these may
       not apply to your particular aircraft.
       16.5.2.2. Disengage the autothrottle if engaged. This will prevent the autothrottle from
       increasing engine thrust.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                               187


     16.5.2.3. Turn on continuous ignition.
     16.5.2.4. Turn on all accessory airbleeds including all air conditioning packs, nacelles,
     and wing anti-ice. This will provide an additional engine stall margin by reducing engine
     pressure.
     16.5.2.5. It may become necessary to shut down and then restart engines to prevent
     exceeding engine temperature limits. Also, volcanic ash may block the pitot system
     resulting in unreliable airspeed indications.
  16.5.3. You should make every attempt to remain on the upwind side of the volcano.
  Flightcrews who have encountered volcanic ash clouds provided the following indicators to
  help you recognize this hazard.
     16.5.3.1. Smoke or dust appearing in the cockpit.
     16.5.3.2. An acrid odor similar to electrical smoke.
     16.5.3.3. At night, St. Elmo’s fire or other static discharges accompanied by a bright
     orange glow in the engine inlets.
     16.5.3.4. At night, or in dark clouds, landing lights cast dark distinct shadows in ash
     clouds, unlike the fuzzy, indistinct shadows cast against weather clouds.
     16.5.3.5. Multiple engine malfunctions, such as compressor stalls, increasing exhaust gas
     temperatures, torching from tailpipes and flameouts.
     16.5.3.6. A fire warning in forward cargo areas.
 188                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


                                          Chapter 17

                           NUMERICAL WEATHER MODELS

17.1. Numerical Weather Prediction.
   17.1.1. Numerical Weather Prediction (NWP) uses the power of computers to make a
   forecast. Complex computer programs, also known as forecast models, run on
   supercomputers and provide predictions of many atmospheric variables such as temperature,
   pressure, wind, and precipitation. A forecaster examines how the features predicted by the
   computer will interact to produce the day's weather.
   17.1.2. The NWP method is flawed in that the equations used by the models to simulate the
   atmosphere are not precise, and the solutions to the equations themselves are only
   approximations to the exact solutions, which are impossible to calculate. This leads to some
   error in the predictions. In addition, there are many gaps in the initial data since we do not
   receive many weather observations from areas in the mountains or over the ocean. If the
   initial state is not completely known, the computer's prediction of how that initial state will
   evolve will not be entirely accurate.
   17.1.3. Despite these flaws, numerical weather prediction is quite adept at forecasting day-
   to-day weather changes.
   17.1.4. Numerical weather forecasting is based on a set of rather straightforward ideas:
       17.1.4.1. Weather elements are interconnected, so that as change occurs at one location,
       this in turn influences the weather elements at other locations
       17.1.4.2. Weather elements change sequentially through time, so that if you know the
       temperature at one location, it is then possible to 'predict' the temperature at the same
       place in some subsequent time (i.e., 24 hours from now).
       17.1.4.3. It is possible to use computers and simultaneous equations dealing with
       changes in atmospheric components, to model what the changes in weather elements will
       be over time.

Figure 17.1. NWP Grid Example.




   17.1.5. In the diagram above (Figure 17.1), the surface of the Earth has been broken up into
   a set of equally spaced grids, with a weather station at the center of each grid. If the
   temperature at A changes, this will influence the temperature at B, C, and D. These changes
   in temperature will in turn influence the temperature at A. As you now know this
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                     189


   formulation is too simplistic, since a change in temperature will also produce a change in
   pressure, winds, humidity, etc.
   17.1.6. A numerical weather prediction model works by using a previous, short-range model
   forecast that has been corrected by observations to closely resemble the actual atmospheric
   state at model run time. A large set of partial differential equations is then integrated forward
   in time to create a forecast of the future atmospheric state for grid points that are situated
   across the model domain.
   17.1.7. Because our surface weather, in the larger scale, is 'controlled' by the upper air
   circulation, most numerical models deal with the Earth not just as a set of grids on the Earth's
   surface, but extend the grids upward into the atmosphere, dividing these columns of the
   atmosphere into squares or cubes. Some models use as many as 50-90 different levels of the
   atmosphere in their analysis. The image below (Figure 17.2) depicts how you might
   visualize one of these grids, composed of vertically stacked cubes.

Figure 17.2. NWP Extended Grid Example.




   17.1.8. Obviously this method of calculating the changes in a number of different weather
   elements with their myriad interactions, for a globe or a region divided into 20-100 different
   grids with 50-90 different levels, requires the use of high speed supercomputers.
17.2. Model Types and Limitations.
   17.2.1. As supercomputers have become more powerful, NWP models have become a more
   common feature in forecasting. Some are intended to forecast a limited range of weather
   elements for rather specific locations, or conditions, while others are intended to give the best
   predictions possible for the globe or some particular continent. In general we subdivide the
   models into groups on the basis of the length of time they forecast into the future, short term,
   usually less than 24 hours, intermediate term, often 24-72 hours and longer term, 72 hours
   out to a year. Another way to categorize the models is on the basis of their aerial scope or
   extent, and therefore you will often find discussions of ―regional‖ (or mesoscale) and
   ―global‖ models. You’ll often see models characterized by their resolution, or the size of the
   grid they use in their computational process. It’s important to note that model resolution and
   type impacts the range of phenomena a given model will be able to forecast. A global model
 190                                                          AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


   will generally be good at forecasting large-scale atmospheric features over a relatively long
   period of time, while a mesoscale model with smaller grid boxes will be able to simulate
   finer-scale atmospheric features (e.g., thunderstorms), but not necessarily accurately because
   of the increased complexity of smaller-scale flows.
17.3. Validating a Numerical Forecast.
   17.3.1. To validate the accuracy of a model, you have to compare the forecast products with
   similar products that reflected actual conditions for the forecast period. The comparison
   method here is fairly crude (i.e., using primarily visual analyses of the patterns, but it does
   seem to yield reasonable results).

Figure 17.3. 500 Mb Geopotential Surface Maps,




The two maps above represent the forecast and actual 500 Mb geopotential surfaces for Nov.
25th. As you can see the two surfaces exhibit the same overall form, a trough, another in the
Midwest with a weak ridge in between. The forecast location of the western trough, with its
lowest values near Western Washington, and its extension southward to near the California,
Nevada and Arizona border is also quite accurate. The actual location of the eastern trough
seems to be shifted slightly to the east of its forecast location, however again the low center is
nearly on target, over Lake Superior. Finally the location of the major areas of convergence,
across Southern California, and northward across Lower Michigan seem to be good fits.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                   191


Figure 17.4. The Jet Stream Maps.




The jet stream forecast seemed to work out fairly well. Again the trough and ridge locations are
fairly good, and the areas of maximum convergence and highest jet stream winds are almost
exactly where they were forecast to be, relative to the ridge axis. The California jet streak is
slightly eastward of its forecast location, lying more along the western border of the state than
along the coast. Again the model seemed to have problems forecasting air flow over the ridge,
with the overall flow being further south, and somewhat stronger than it was supposed to be in
this area. Conversely the maximum winds forecast for the jet streaks are remarkably close, the
forecast for the western area was 150 knots, the observed value 145 knots, while the maximum
value for the eastern jet streak was forecast to be 120 knots, and the observed value was 121
knots.


Figure 17.5. Temperature Forecast Maps.
 192                                                         AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


         The overall form of the forecast temperature map seems to conform fairly well to the
actual observed temperature pattern. There is a pool of cold air over North Central Canada, with
a lobe extending southward into the Central Plains states. Along the East Coast a tongue of
warm air extends from Florida northward into Quebec. The primary departures of the forecast
pattern from the observed pattern seems to be that the central lobe of cold air is somewhat west
of its predicted location, and the warm air flow across the southwest up into Southern Idaho isn't
as strong as it had been forecast to be.

Figure 17.6. Surface Pressures.




The overall surface pressure patterns forecast seemed to be fairly good. The observed centers of
the low in the west and the one in the Midwest are both within 100 miles of their forecast
location. Again the southern high pressure system, in between the two lows didn't fare quite as
well, with its actual central location being quite a bit to the east of its forecast location.
The forecast for the relative intensity of the two low pressure cells was amazingly accurate, the
western low was forecast to have a central low pressure of 987, its actual pressure was 995,
while the eastern low was forecast to have a central low pressure of 995 and its observed
pressure was 990. The forecast for the central pressure for the high was 1020; its actual value
was 1017.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                     193


Figure 17.7. Atmospheric Moisture and Precipitation.




The overall pattern of the forecast moisture generally conformed closely to the observed clouds
and rainfall. The primary areas of lifting and precipitation were in the correct places, relative to
the pressure cells and pressure centers. In both the west and the east the major areas of
convergence were shifted more to the east than forecast. For example the area of major
convergence and precipitation in the east was forecast to be along the western border of Ohio at
1200Z, when in fact it was along the eastern border of that state. As noted in the initial segment
of this discussion, on the Numeric Models page, Charleston received .75 of an inch of rain as the
line of convergence passed across the area, which was exactly in the middle of the forecast rage
for precipitation from this system. The more rapid movement of the convergence band also is
reflected in the cloud cover recorded in the Midwest, with the forecast indicating clouds
extending as far south as the Iowa - Missouri border at 1200Z when in fact the wrap around
clouds extended all the way to the Ohio River in Southern Illinois.
   17.3.2. In general the MRF model did a credible job of forecasting in this case study. Some
   parameters were forecast better than others, but overall forecast weather phenomena were
   generally where they were supposed to be, at the intensity they were actually observed to be.
17.4. Summary. Numerical models have become the mainstay of weather forecasting. The
case study we examined for Nov. 25th is a good example of why, with the output of the models
being remarkably close to the actual conditions that were observed on that day. In this example,
the model was accurate, but was it just a ―good day‖ or is this an anomaly? This cannot be
known, but overall, forecasters have come to depend on numerical forecasts to make their lives
easier, but as you know, even with numerical models, forecasters still have days when their
forecasts are obviously in error.
 194                                                       AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


                                          Chapter 18

                                     SPACE WEATHER

18.1. Introduction. Although exact definitions vary, space is broadly defined as the region
above the earth’s lower atmosphere where an object can maintain an orbit around the planet. An
object can just maintain orbit—albeit temporary—starting around 100 kilometers in altitude.
Space weather describes the phenomena in space that affect the earth, its atmosphere, and our
technological systems that operate on or around the earth. Space weather is a consequence of
solar activity and its interaction with the earth’s magnetic field and atmosphere. Space weather
impacts many systems, including communications, radar, navigation, electronics, power grids,
spacecraft, aircraft, and even the human body. Impacts to these systems occur in orbit,
throughout the atmosphere, at the earth’s surface, and even below the surface.
18.2. Regions of Space Weather. Space weather is divided into several distinct regions based
on what forces dominate: the sun, the medium between the earth and sun, the volume dominated
by the earth’s magnetic field, the upper atmosphere (above about 50 kilometers in altitude) and
the lower atmosphere (the extent of aviation operations).
   18.2.1. The Sun. The origin of space weather phenomena in the near earth environment lies
   primarily with the sun, the closest star to Earth. The sun is more than a million times larger
   than the earth and over 300 thousand times more massive. Three quarters of the sun consists
   of hydrogen; the remainder consists of helium and trace amounts of heavier elements such as
   oxygen, carbon, iron, neon, nitrogen, silicon, magnesium, sulfur, and calcium.
       18.2.1.1. At the solar core, intense pressures and high temperatures due to gravitational
       forces cause hydrogen to fuse into helium (Figure 18.1). This nuclear fusion process
       fuels the sun, converting over 4 trillion tons of mass into radiation every second. By
       repeated absorption and emission, this radiation eventually works its way outward
       through the radiative zone. Then, by heat-driven mixing in the convective zone, energy is
       brought to the solar surface, where heat and light escape into space from what we observe
       as the photosphere (Figure 18.2).
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                        195


Figure 18.1. Nuclear Fusion at Solar Core.




Figure 18.2. Basic Solar Structure.




       18.2.1.2. Like the earth, the sun rotates on an axis. While the earth takes about 24 hours
       to rotate once, the sun takes about a month to rotate once. Actually, since the sun is not
       solid, it rotates at different rates. At the solar equator it takes about 25 earth days to rotate
       once, and at the solar poles it takes about 35 days (Figure 18.3).
 196                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


Figure 18.3. Solar Rotation.




       18.2.1.3. Unlike the earth, which has a relatively fixed magnetic field, the sun constantly
       generates and rearranges its magnetic field into complex structures. These ever changing
       structures trap solar material above the photosphere in the region called the solar corona.
       In Figure 18.4, note that looping magnetic fields above the photosphere can be seen by
       striations in coronal material. The image is taken in extreme ultraviolet and therefore
       shows that the photosphere, below the corona, appears black in the image, as the
       photosphere does not emit extreme ultraviolet or x-rays. In the corona, the thinly
       distributed atoms are heated and ionized—that is, one or more electrons are stripped from
       an atom, leaving a positively charged ion. Since the hydrogen atom consists of a
       positively-charged proton surrounded by one negatively-charged electron, a hydrogen ion
       is simply a proton—the nucleus of the hydrogen atom. The helium atom consists of two
       protons bound to two neutrons, which are surrounded by two electrons. There are two
       types of helium ions: one with only a single electron removed, and the other with both
       electrons removed, leaving the helium nucleus. A helium nucleus is also called an alpha
       particle. The collection of positively-charged ions and their negatively-charged electrons
       is called plasma and is considered the fourth state of matter (solid, liquid, and gas being
       the first three). It differs from a gas in that it can become magnetized and easily conducts
       electricity. Figure 18.5 shows hydrogen ions (protons) and helium nuclei that make up
       most solar plasma in the corona. Arrows show independent motion of electrons (negative
       charges) and ions (positive charges).
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                     197


Figure 18.4. Solar Magnetic Field Traps Solar Material in Corona.




Figure 18.5. Solar Plasma (hydrogen nuclei (protons), helium nuclei (alpha particles), and
free electrons).




       18.2.1.4. Because of its heat, solar plasma in the corona emits ultraviolet, x-ray radiation;
       because of its low density, it also emits microwave and radio waves. This coronal
       radiation contributes to the light and heat already output from the photosphere. In Figure
       18.6, note that 99% of the sun’s energy comes as light, heat (infrared), and ultraviolet
       radiation from the photosphere. The remaining 1% comes as radio, microwave, extreme
       ultraviolet radiation, and x-rays from the corona. Although coronal radiation is a small
       fraction of the total solar electromagnetic output, it turns out that x-rays and radio waves
 198                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


       have the most impact on technological systems here on earth and in orbit. Furthermore,
       radiation at these wavelengths varies the most during solar activity, while light and heat
       output remains virtually constant.

Figure 18.6. Solar Electromagnetic Spectrum (note scale change at longer wavelengths).




   18.2.2. The Interplanetary Medium.
       18.2.2.1. Hot plasma in the solar corona constantly leaks outward away from the sun,
       generating the thin flow of charged particles (electrons, protons, and alpha particles) that
       we call the solar wind, which carries with it a small part of the solar magnetic field. The
       solar wind streams radially away from the sun at about 400 kilometers per second, and
       because the sun rotates, the magnetic field embedded in the solar wind is stretched into a
       spiral shape. This spiral magnetic field is called the interplanetary magnetic field—IMF
       for short, as illustrated in Figure 18.7, where the sun is located at the center.

Figure 18.7. The Interplanetary Magnetic Field (IMF).




       18.2.2.2. The solar wind sweeps past the earth and dominates most of the space in the
       solar system, far beyond the orbit of Pluto. However, keep in mind how thin the solar
       wind really is, despite its name, which connotes flags blowing in a stiff gale. The density
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                   199


      of the solar wind ranges from a few to a hundred particles per cubic centimeter, while the
      air you are breathing right now is a hundred thousand trillion times denser. In fact, the
      best vacuum chambers found in physics labs are hard pressed to create such low
      pressures and densities exhibited by the solar wind.
      18.2.2.3. The interplanetary medium is also infiltrated by galactic cosmic rays, which do
      not result from solar activity but instead originate from very distant events such as
      exploding stars and black holes consuming stars. Galactic cosmic rays primarily consist
      of electrons and ions—protons mostly—that travel close to the speed of light. They streak
      through the solar system from every direction at a generally steady rate. Incidentally,
      other interesting phenomena that occur in the interplanetary medium are rarely
      considered space weather phenomena per se because impact is so limited. These include
      meteor showers, asteroids, and comets. These are more properly considered astronomical
      events, which may affect space weather in some limited fashion.
   18.2.3. The Earth’s Magnetosphere.
      18.2.3.1. The earth has a relatively fixed magnetic field, which is shaped like a dipole.
      This means that lines of magnetic force emanate from the south polar region, loop out
      into space, and converge into the north polar region. Projected in three dimensions, the
      dipole magnetic field of the earth resembles a donut out to about 50 thousand kilometers
      (Figure 18.8). Beyond this, the magnetic field of the earth is weak enough to be swept
      aside by the solar wind, which carries with it the IMF, a weak magnetic field pulled from
      the sun’s outer corona.

Figure 18.8. Inner Magnetosphere of the Earth.




      18.2.3.2. Solar wind action against the earth’s magnetic field compresses the earth’s field
      on the sunlit hemisphere, but drags it millions of kilometers in the anti-sunward direction,
      forming a bullet or comet shaped cavity, wherein the earth’s magnetic field dominates
      (Figure 18.9). This cavity, despite its shape, is called the magnetosphere. The forward
      edge of the magnetosphere—on the sun side—is called the nose of the magnetosphere.
      The elongated portion behind the earth is called the tail of the magnetosphere, or simply
      the magnetotail.
 200                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


Figure 18.9. Solar Wind Interaction with Earth’s Magnetosphere.




       18.2.3.3. The magnetosphere deflects the solar wind—and the embedded IMF—around
       the earth, generally keeping solar particles out of the magnetosphere. However, where the
       earth’s magnetosphere is especially weak, solar wind particles can be entrained. This
       occurs in the magnetotail, where solar wind plasma can be turbulently mixed into the
       magnetosphere. Charged particles can also enter the magnetosphere through small areas
       called polar cusps (or polar clefts), which are located above the earth’s magnetic poles
       toward the forward edge of the magnetosphere.
       18.2.3.4. Charged particles remain trapped in the earth’s magnetosphere unless they
       collide into the earth’s atmosphere or manage to escape down the magnetotail during the
       turbulent mixing with the solar wind that regularly occurs there.
   18.2.4. The Ionosphere.
       18.2.4.1. The earth’s atmosphere can be organized according to temperature (see Figure
       1.2). The troposphere is the lowest layer and is characterized by decreasing temperatures
       with height, up to about 12 kilometers (40,000 feet) in altitude. Much of the sun’s light
       and heat radiation is absorbed at the earth’s surface, which in turn acts as a heat source
       for the troposphere and virtually all meteorological processes that occur therein.
       18.2.4.2. Above this lies the stratosphere, which is characterized by a slow increase in
       temperature with height, from about 12 kilometers to 50 kilometers (40,000 – 165,000
       feet) in altitude. Aircraft fly their highest in the bottom portion of this layer. The upper
       portion of this layer is heated by absorption of ultraviolet radiation from the sun. This
       absorption process involves the creation and destruction of ozone (O3, the tri-atomic form
       of oxygen).
       18.2.4.3. Above this lies the mesosphere, perhaps the least understood layer of the
       atmosphere because it’s too high for aircraft and too low for satellites. This layer is
       characterized by another decrease of temperature with height, from about 50 kilometers
       to 85 kilometers (165,000 – 280,000 feet) in altitude.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                  201


      18.2.4.4. Above this lies the thermosphere, which is characterized by another increase in
      temperature with height, above about 85 kilometers (280,000 feet) in altitude. This
      increase in temperature is due to the absorption of ultraviolet radiation and x-rays from
      the sun. In the process of absorbing this radiation, some (not all) nitrogen and oxygen
      molecules in the thermosphere become ionized (Figure 18.10). The resulting ions and
      their free electrons create a layer we call the ionosphere. This layer of partially-ionized
      air stretches from about 70 kilometers above the earth’s surface (the upper part of the
      mesosphere), through the thermosphere, and thins upward into space.

Figure 18.10. Ionospheric Plasma Environment (O2, N2, their ions, and electrons).




      18.2.4.5. The principle characteristic of the ionosphere is its capacity to refract—or
      deflect—radio waves. As we utilize radio frequencies in numerous applications, the
      ionosphere can be an important tool or a debilitating obstacle.
      18.2.4.6. As illustrated in Figure 18.11, the ionosphere is divided into several layers,
      each characterized by unique chemistry and physics that is driven by atmospheric
      density, solar radiation, and type of atmospheric molecule or atom present in that layer.
 202                                                          AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


Figure 18.11. Ionospheric Layers.




       18.2.4.7. The lowest layer—the D-layer, or D region—resides between about 70 and 90
       kilometers in altitude. In this layer, solar x-rays ionize molecular oxygen (O2) and
       nitrogen (N2), including nitric oxide (NO). However, at night the electrons and ions in
       this layer quickly recombine into neutral molecules. Therefore, the D-layer exists only
       when the sun is up.
       18.2.4.8. The next ionospheric layer—the E-layer, or E region—is located between 90
       and 140 kilometers in altitude. In this layer, solar ultraviolet radiation ionizes O2, N2, and
       NO; but because the atmosphere is thinner at this higher altitude, electrons and ions
       recombine at a slower rate at night. Thus the E-layer maintains itself throughout the
       night, although it weakens considerably. Only at high latitudes (e.g. Alaska, Canada,
       Arctic Ocean), during the weeks and months of winter darkness, does the E-layer
       completely disappear.
       18.2.4.9. The next ionospheric layer—the F-layer, or F region—is located between 140
       and 300 kilometers in altitude. In this layer, solar ultraviolet radiation primarily ionizes
       atomic oxygen (O), but the extremely thin atmosphere at this altitude inhibits the electron
       and ion recombination process, so compared to the entire ionospheric structure this layer
       remains the most ionized, day and night. A unique feature of the F-layer is that it splits
       into two distinct ionized levels during the day. The lower is the F1-layer, the upper is the
       F2-layer.
   18.2.5. Radio Refraction in the Ionosphere.
       18.2.5.1. Similar to the way visible light refracts (or ―bends‖) when it passes through a
       transparent medium such as water or glass, radio waves refract when they pass through
       the ionosphere. This can be useful…or it can be a nuisance. The key is compensating for
       its effects, especially when it is changing, for example during sunrise or sunset. The
       various radio and radar bands interact with the earth’s surface, the atmosphere, and the
       ionosphere differently.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                203


     18.2.5.2. Low Frequency (LF) signals range in frequency between 30 kilohertz and 300
     kilohertz (kHz). The ionosphere strongly absorbs LF signals; however, LF waves hug the
     surface of the earth, which made them useful as a navigation aid (e.g. LORAN—Long
     Range Navigation) until recently, as GPS (Global Positioning System) navigation has
     replaced this function.
     18.2.5.3. Medium Frequency (MF) signals range in frequency between 300 kHz and 3
     megahertz (MHz). Like LF signals, MF signals hug the earth’s surface and are used in
     long range navigation near the surface—ideal for maritime and low-level aviation
     operations (e.g. NDB—Non-Directional Beacon; GMDSS—Global Maritime Distress
     Safety System). Incidentally, the AM radio band (Amplitude Modulation) uses MF
     signals; however, during the day these communication signals are absorbed by the
     ionospheric D-layer and so their range is limited. At night AM radio can transmit
     hundreds of kilometers because MF signals refract in the higher ionospheric layers with
     little absorption. The time around sunrise and sunset pose special problems for MF
     communication signals as the ionosphere is changing rapidly during these times.
     18.2.5.4. High Frequency (HF) signals also known as Shortwave, range in frequency
     between 3 MHz and 30 MHz. Since the ionosphere easily refracts HF signals back to the
     earth, this radio band is especially valuable for transmitting signals thousands of
     kilometers, around the curvature of the earth. It is useful for communicating across
     continents, across oceans, and into high latitudes (the Arctic and Antarctic) where
     satellite communications is limited or impossible. HF signals rely heavily on ionospheric
     conditions and are particularly susceptible to ionospheric disturbances.
     18.2.5.5. Very High Frequency (VHF) signals range in frequency between 30 MHz and
     300 MHz. The ionosphere refracts VHF signals somewhat, but not enough to reliably
     transmit a signal over the horizon back to the earth’s surface; therefore, VHF signals are
     limited to shorter distances between two points on the earth’s surface and also used for
     satellite communications. Aviation use of the VHF radio band includes VOR (VHF
     Omni-directional Radio Range, DME (Distance Measuring Equipment), TACAN
     (Tactical Air Navigation System), ILS (Instrument Landing System), and ATC (Air
     Traffic Control) communications. Incidentally, the FM radio band (Frequency
     Modulation) and some television broadcasting use VHF signals. The following three
     radio bands fall under the broad definition of the microwave radio band.
     18.2.5.6. Ultra High Frequency (UHF) signals range in frequency between 300 MHz and
     3 gigahertz (GHz). The ionosphere affects UHF less than VHF, so this band is ideal for
     line-of-sight (LOS) communications, including satellite communications and GPS
     navigation signals. Many radars operate in the UHF radio band (e.g. the L-band and S-
     band). Incidentally, some television broadcasting use UHF signals.
     18.2.5.7. Super High Frequency (SHF) signals range in frequency between 3 GHz and
     30 GHz and are heavily used for satellite communications, primarily because the
     ionosphere has little effect on SHF signals. On the other hand, heavy precipitation—rain,
     snow, and hail—can attenuate such signals; therefore LOS signals between two points at
     the earth’s surface can have limited range. Many radars also operate in the SHF radio
     band (e.g. the IEEE C-band, X-band, and K-bands), especially for use in tracking objects
     in orbit.
 204                                                         AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


       18.2.5.8. Extremely High Frequency (EHF) signals range in frequency between 30 GHz
       and 300 GHz. Again, ideal for satellite communications as the ionosphere has virtually
       no effect on EHF signals. However, precipitation, clouds, and even water vapor make
       portions of this radio band of little use.
18.3. Solar Activity. The sun is not static sphere of hot plasma, but instead has its own
―seasons‖ of activity. The sun exhibits differential rotation—that is, it rotates at different rates
like fluid in a whirlpool.
   18.3.1. Active Regions. At the solar equator it takes about 25 earth days to rotate once, and
   at the solar poles it takes about 35 days. In consequence of this differential rotation, magnetic
   fields that erupt from the solar surface can become twisted and subsequently strengthened in
   some regions. Figure 18.12 shows how complex these field patterns are. The stronger
   magnetic fields on the photosphere drive away the hotter solar material, leaving relatively
   cooler areas on the photosphere. These dark patches are called sunspots and indicate the
   presence of strong magnetic fields erupting from the solar interior (Figure 18.13). The
   darkest center of a sunspot is called the umbra, and it is where strong magnetic fields are
   vertical to the solar surface. The surrounding less dark area of the sunspot is called the
   penumbra; it is where strong magnetic fields are more horizontal to the solar surface.

Figure 18.12. Solar Magnetic Fields.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                  205


Figure 18.13. Sunspots on the Photosphere (whole disk image and close-up of sunspots).




   18.3.2. Solar Flares (X-ray Flares, Radio Bursts, Energetic Proton Events). When strong
   magnetic fields of active regions become tangled in complex knot-like structures, the fields
   can suddenly ―snap‖ in order to reorient themselves into less complex configurations. Such
   an explosive event is called a solar flare, which generates a flash of x-rays, bursts of radio
   waves, and energetic charged particles—particularly protons (Figures 18.14 through 18.16).
   If these important space weather phenomena are directed toward the earth, they significantly
   impact technological systems that operate on the earth and in orbit above. The flash of x-
   rays—or x-ray flare—lasts only tens of minutes, as does the burst of radio waves, but the
   energetic protons can become trapped in the earth’s magnetosphere and persist for days after
   a solar flare.

Figure 18.14. Solar Flare (x-ray image and traces for two x-ray wavelengths).
 206                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


Figure 18.15. Radio Burst Trace.




Figure 18.16. Energetic Proton Trace (showing three different proton energy levels).




   18.3.3. Coronal Mass Ejections (CME).
       18.3.3.1. Sometimes complex magnetic fields in the solar corona can reorient in such a
       way that a batch of solar plasma is ejected into space—a coronal mass ejection, or CME
       for short. Figure 18.17 shows such an ejection: an occulting disk covers the brilliant light
       of the sun (indicated by a white circle) while the much dimmer CME appears in the
       overexposed image at the lower left quadrant. A CME can be triggered by a solar flare,
       but not necessarily. A CME injects into the solar wind major discontinuities of density,
       speed, temperature, and magnetic fields. Such a discontinuity in the IMF is illustrated in
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                    207


      Figure 18.18 (along with other discontinuities). These discontinuities propagate outward
      away from the sun, and if they are oriented toward the earth, they strike the
      magnetosphere, and important consequences result for a number of technological
      systems.
      18.3.3.2. A CME can erupt from the sun within tens of minutes to hours; however, it
      takes another two days or so for the full effects of solar wind discontinuities to be evident
      at the earth.

Figure 18.17. Coronal Mass Ejection (CME).




Figure 18.18. IMF Discontinuity Due to CME.




   18.3.4. Coronal Holes.
      18.3.4.1. Within the complex mix of magnetic fields in and above the solar surface, large
      areas of magnetic fields leading straight into space can form (see Figure 18.12, above top
      of solar disk). These areas allow charged particles—especially electrons—to accelerate
      away from the sun. As viewed in x-ray or extreme ultraviolet imagery, these regions
 208                                                       AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


       appear dark compared to the glow of hot plasma trapped in other places in the corona.
       Thus the description: coronal hole, which can be seen in the ultraviolet image of the sun
       in Figure 18.19. The stream of fast particles that originate from coronal holes causes
       significant discontinuities in the solar wind. Such features are labeled by other black
       arrows in Figure 18.18. These solar wind discontinuities, if directed toward the earth,
       strike the magnetosphere, and important consequences result for a number of
       technological systems.
       18.3.4.2. Coronal holes often last weeks to months, but because the sun rotates, the fast
       particles streaming from one particular coronal hole tend to periodically sweep past the
       earth every four weeks or so.

Figure 18.19. Coronal Hole Seen In Ultraviolet Imagery (large dark blob at center of disk).




   18.3.5. Geomagnetic Storms.
       18.3.5.1. As the solar wind deflects around the earth, it compresses the sunward side of
       the earth’s magnetosphere, flows around the sides, and drags it out the back side, forming
       the magnetotail. Discontinuities of density, speed, temperature, and magnetic field
       embedded in the solar wind disrupt the magnetosphere, causing fluctuations in magnetic
       field strength and orientation—a geomagnetic storm. These solar wind discontinuities are
       caused by CMEs, coronal holes, and other IMF features.
       18.3.5.2. When the earth’s magnetosphere is thus disturbed, solar wind plasma—
       consisting mostly of electrons and protons—is turbulently mixed into the magnetotail.
       Surges of these charged particles accelerate along magnetic field lines toward the earth,
       where they mix with ions and electrons already trapped in the magnetosphere (Figure
       18.20). A fluctuating magnetosphere during a geomagnetic storm accelerates trapped
       charged particles—chiefly electrons—to high energies. Some of these particles funnel
       into the polar regions—following magnetic field lines that converge there—and then
       collide with molecules in the thermosphere. We can see this effect as the aurora (Figure
       18.21). In the Arctic, these shifting glowing curtains of light are called the aurora
       borealis, or northern lights. Less observed, but occurring with equal intensity and
       frequency in the Antarctic are the aurora australis—or southern lights.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                209


Figure 18.20. Geomagnetic Storm Diagram.




Figure 18.21. Aurora Viewed from the Space Shuttle.




      18.3.5.3. The earth’s magnetic field axis is tilted about 11° from the earth’s rotational
      axis. This places the magnetic north pole somewhere northeast of Canada in the Arctic
      Ocean and the magnetic south pole over the Australian side of Antarctica. It is thought
      that gradual circulation changes in the earth’s outer liquid iron core cause the magnetic
      poles to wander slowly over the years. During a geomagnetic storm the aurora forms
      crowns of light, the auroral ovals, which are centered on the magnetic poles (Figure
      18.22)—not the geographic poles. That is why the aurora borealis is observed more in
      Alaska and Canada than at the same geographic latitudes in Scandinavia and Siberia. The
      more severe the geomagnetic storm, the larger the auroral ovals become, and the further
      south the northern lights are observed. During especially severe geomagnetic storms, the
      aurora is observed as far south as Mexico and Cuba!
 210                                                        AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


       18.3.5.4. The disrupting force of solar wind discontinuities usually last for a number of
       hours; however, the residual effects on the magnetosphere can persist for many hours—
       even days—longer.

Figure 18.22. Auroral Ovals (Aurora Borealis and Australis).




   18.3.6. The Van Allen Radiation Belts.
       18.3.6.1. Not all energetic charged particles trapped in the earth’s magnetosphere end up
       striking the atmosphere, as evidenced by aurora. Only a fraction squeak through to the
       atmosphere at high latitudes to cause aurora. The rest remained trapped for
       months…even years. In fact, charged particles trapped in the magnetosphere dart back
       and forth between the converging magnetic field lines in the polar regions, filling donut-
       shaped regions surrounding the earth called the Van Allen radiation belts, named after the
       man who first proposed and proved their existence. These trapped particles are so
       energetic that they’re considered particle radiation because they penetrate and interact
       with spacecraft materials (and humans) in the same way that x-ray and gamma radiation
       can.
       18.3.6.2. There are two distinct radiation belts (Figure 18.23). The outer Van Allen
       radiation belt is populated by solar wind material that the magnetosphere has captured in
       the magnetotail. The strength and geometry of the magnetic field in the outer belt serves
       to accelerate electrons. The inner belt—the bottom of which interacts with the top of the
       earth’s atmosphere—also contains energetic charged particles, but their origin might be
       surprising. The nearly constant shower of cosmic rays that bathe the earth from all
       directions collide with atmospheric molecules and scatter their atomic nuclei. The protons
       liberated in these collisions follow magnetic field lines into space and populate the inner
       Van Allen radiation belt.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                   211


Figure 18.23. Van Allen Radiation Belts.




   18.3.7. The 27-day Solar Rotation Cycle.
      18.3.7.1. As viewed from the earth, which is revolving around the sun in the same
      direction that the sun is rotating, the solar equator rotates in about 27 earth days, though
      at higher solar latitudes, it takes longer. Thus many features found on the sun tend to
      exhibit a 27-day periodicity.
      18.3.7.2. For example, an active region appears on the near side of the sun for a couple
      weeks, and then rotates out of view. One anticipates that if the active region maintains
      itself, it will appear again after a couple weeks.
      18.3.7.3. The high-speed plasma streams in the solar wind associated with coronal holes
      also recur at this frequency.
   18.3.8. The 11-year Solar Activity Cycle.
      18.3.8.1. The occurrence of active regions, associated sunspots, flares, and CMEs is not
      constant. The sun exhibits an approximate 11-year cycle of these phenomena. We’ve
      noticed this cycle for centuries (Figure 18.24). The unusual (and as of yet explained) lack
      of activity and associated sunspots during the last half of the 17th century is commonly
      called the Maunder Minimum.
      18.3.8.2. Solar minimum marks the beginning of a cycle and is characterized by few—if
      any—active regions and associated sunspots. Solar flares are rare and CMEs are less
      frequent. However, coronal holes are larger and more persistent.
      18.3.8.3. For a few years following solar minimum, active regions, their associated
      sunspots, and flares increase in frequency and intensity. This is due to the differential
      rotation of the sun ―winding‖ up magnetic field below the solar surface, which eventually
      emerges through the photosphere as active regions, with its associated sunspots. This
      activity at its height—often defined by the maximum number of observed sunspots—is
      called solar maximum, which can last months to a couple years. Then, over the course of
      several years following solar maximum, activity subsides and returns to solar minimum,
      which marks the next cycle start.
 212                                                       AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


       18.3.8.4. As you can see from Figure 18.24, solar minimum occurred during 2008-2009;
       solar maximum is expected sometime around 2012 or 2013, when we expect frequent
       flares, CMEs, and energetic proton events.

Figure 18.24. 11-Year Solar Cycle (as of December 2010).




18.4. Space Weather Impacts. Direct space weather impacts to aviation include degradation to
various radio signals and certain radiation hazards. Radio signals include communication, radar,
and navigation functions.
   18.4.1. HF Signal Impacts.
       18.4.1.1. Communications and radars operating in the HF radio band are especially
       susceptible to ionospheric disturbances. Since HF is usually used for long-range
       applications, it is important not to neglect that impacts to HF signals may occur anywhere
       along the line of transmission, not just at the sites of transmission or reception.
       18.4.1.2. Dawn and Dusk. The times around sunset and sunrise pose particular
       challenges to HF operations because it is during these times that the lower ionosphere
       responds to the change in solar radiation. HF transmitters often use separate daytime and
       nighttime frequencies to accommodate both of these ionospheric conditions. Daytime
       transmission frequencies are always higher than nighttime. During dawn and dusk, users
       often vacillate between daytime and nighttime frequencies to obtain best reception until
       the ionosphere settles down for the day (or night), during which a single frequency can be
       used. The graph in Figure 18.25 shows the change in useable HF frequencies throughout
       the day for a specific antenna configuration. The HF window in this case is bound by the
       maximum useable frequency (MUF) and the lowest useable frequency (LUF), where the
       frequency of optimum transmission (FOT) is also indicated.
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                213


Figure 18.25. HF Window Throughout the Day.




      18.4.1.3. X-ray Flares. When the sun produces an x-ray flare, the ionosphere on the
      earth’s dayside hemisphere is disrupted. The lower ionosphere—the D-layer—becomes
      enhanced enough that it absorbs HF signals. A weak x-ray flare can cause the D-layer to
      absorb the lower part of the HF radio band; a moderate flare can additionally absorb the
      middle part of the radio band; and a powerful flare can absorb the entire HF radio band,
      even the lower part of the VHF radio band. Such an event is called an HF blackout or
      shortwave fade, and depending on how intense the x-ray flare is and how long it lasts, HF
      signals can be absorbed for tens of minutes to many hours. But remember, this type of
      impact to HF communications only occurs during the day, when the ionosphere is
      exposed to solar radiation. Figure 18.26 shows the extent an x-ray flare can affect HF
      signals across the globe. Degradation is indicated by attenuation in decibels

Figure 18.26. D-region Absorption Map During a Solar X-ray Flare.




      18.4.1.4. Geomagnetic Storms (Aurora). As magnificent as the aurora looks, it is also a
      major problem to HF users. The energetic particles that enter the atmosphere turbulently
 214                                                          AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


       disrupt the entire vertical structure of the ionosphere. This causes HF signals to split,
       refract in all directions, absorb, and scintillate. Scintillation is the rapid random change in
       signal intensity, direction, phase, and frequency—certainly an undesired effect.
       Ionospheric disruptions during geomagnetic storms can last hours to a few days. It is also
       important to know that the ionosphere in the auroral regions always tends to have minor
       irregularities, as illustrated in Figure 18.27; therefore, even during times of quiet
       geomagnetic activity, all transpolar signals have potential for degradation.

Figure 18.27. Auroral Oval Impact to Ionosphere During Average Geomagnetic Activity
(red arrow indicates direction of sun).




       18.4.1.5. Proton Events. In addition to producing a flash of x-rays and radio bursts, a
       solar flare can inject energetic protons into the solar wind. These protons can approach
       the speed of light and can reach the earth in as little time as one hour after a flare occurs.
       Such energetic protons can funnel into the polar cusps of the earth’s magnetosphere (see
       Figure 18.9) and strike the polar atmosphere. These events do not cause aurora, but
       instead enhance the lower ionosphere (D-layer) much like an x-ray flare. Such an
       enhancement causes absorption of HF signals at high latitudes and is called a polar cap
       absorption event (or PCA). Figure 18.28 shows the affected areas during a PCA event
       (the faint violet over the Americas is the normal daytime D-layer enhancement). Figure
       18.29 shows the results of a major solar flare that produced a powerful x-ray flare (the
       bulls eye over the Pacific) and a remarkable PCA event (over the Arctic and Antarctica).
AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012                                                                     215


Figure 18.28. D-region Absorption Map During a Moderate PCA Event.




Figure 18.29. D-region Absorption Map During a Major Solar X-ray Flare and an
Extreme PCA Event (compare to Figure 18. 26and Figure 18.28).




   18.4.2. Solar Radio Interference.
      18.4.2.1. The sun’s corona and active regions are the main source of solar radio output.
      Geometry plays a critical role in how radio noise from the sun impacts signals. The
      alignment of transmitter and receiver to the sun can wash out a signal. For example, it is
      usually futile to attempt locking on to a satellite signal if the sun lies directly behind the
      satellite. Luckily, because the earth is rotating, such chance alignments are not common
      and only last minutes at most. On the other hand, radars are particularly susceptible to
      signal contamination from the sun because radars are transmitting and receiving from a
      wide range of directions, and the sun is often within view. Figure 18.30 shows weather
      radar data with a line of false data due to radio noise from the sun that occurs at the radar
      frequency, in this case 2800 MHz. This is called a sun spike and clearly is not
 216                                                         AFH11-203VI 12 JANUARY 2012


       meteorological in nature. Radar systems generally account for this glitch and so normal
       solar radio noise poses no real impact.

Figure 18.30. Solar Radio Interference on Weather Radar Imagery (streak to the
southeast).




       18.4.2.2. On the other hand, solar flares, CMEs, and other coronal activity can create
       outbursts of radio noise, which can affect communications, navigation, and radar signals
       ranging from HF to SHF radio bands. The radio noise can be so intense that it can
       overwhelm antenna reception through side lobe contamination. Antenna design and
       electronic filters can mitigate some—but not all—of this unwanted radio frequency
       interference, or RFI. During a solar radio burst, receiving antennas on the dayside of the
       earth can suffer complete radio whiteout, effectively rendering such sites blind for tens of
       minutes—or even hours—at a time.
   18.4.3. VHF/UHF Signal Impacts.
       18.4.3.1. Communications and radars operating in the VHF and UHF radio bands are
       less vulnerable to ionospheric disturbances than the HF radio band; however, this
